ذات
قائم بالذات
تركيب
جوهر
عرض
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visibility is only possible for physical bodies.
Correction: The passage argues that visibility is attached to self-subsistence, not to bodyhood. Atoms are self-subsisting and visible, even when not composed as bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does al-Nasafi argue that atoms (jawahir) are visible even when not composed?
A) Because atoms are bodies and all bodies are visible.
B) Because atoms are self-subsisting and visibility is attached to self-subsistence.
C) Because atoms are accidents and accidents are visible.
D) Because atoms are composed of parts that are visible.

Key Takeaway
Visibility is a judgment that depends on self-subsistence, not on being a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that visibility is attached to self-subsistence, not to being a body. Do you actually believe that you will see Allah with your own eyes in the Hereafter? Or do you intellectually accept it but your heart still doubts? Today, confront that doubt: will you sincerely ask Allah to increase your certainty in this reality, or will you let it remain an abstract concept?
?
This passage uses reason to prove a foundational belief. When did you last use your intellect to strengthen your faith, rather than just repeating what others say? Examine yourself: are you relying on blind imitation (taqlid) in your creed? If so, what specific step will you take this week to seek rational evidence for your beliefs, even if it challenges your comfort zone?
?
Al-Nasafi warns that the argument only works against certain opponents. Are you prepared to defend the vision of Allah against those who deny it? If someone asked you tomorrow why you believe God can be seen, would you be able to articulate this proof clearly? Commit to memorizing at least one step of this argument and sharing it with someone before the end of the month.

Al-Maturidi's Method: Scriptural Proofs and Counterargument
Al-Nasafi presents al-Maturidi's method: scriptural proofs and counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Maturidi preferred scriptural proofs over rational arguments for the visibility of God, and how counterargument (mu'arada) is used.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi relies on scriptural proofs and uses counterargument to refute rational objections.

While Al-Nasafi first presented a rational proof, he then reports Imam al-Maturidi's preferred approach: to use scriptural proofs from revelation. When opponents claim rational proofs prevent vision, al-Maturidi advises a counterargument: bodies are seen not because they are bodies but because they are self-subsisting. This lesson explores that method.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Maturidi's preference
Notice that al-Maturidi saw the soundest method to be adhering to scriptural proofs (dala'il sam'iyya).
This is a methodological choice.
2
Understand the counterargument
When opponents say rational proofs prevent vision, they argue that only bodies are seen. Al-Maturidi counters that bodies are seen due to self-subsistence, not bodyhood.
This is mu'arada, a refutation by turning the opponent's reasoning.
3
See the principle from al-Maqalat
Al-Maturidi quotes his own principle: whatever is not seen in this world is an accident, otherwise every self-subsistent thing is seen. God is self-subsistent, so He is seen.
This is not a proof but an analogical comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرؤية واستحالتها مما يرجع إلى الذات، والذات لا تتبدل بالتركيب فلا يتبدل الحكم المتعلق بالذات، وهذا لأن المتبدل بالتركب هو الأحكام المتعلقة بالمقادير والكميات، فإن ما ليس بطويل إذا انضم إلى ما ليس بطويل يصيران جميعا طويلا، وكذا المربع والمثلث والمسدس وغير ذلك من الأشكال، فأما الأحكام المتعلقة بالذوات فلا تبدل لهما بالتركب لانعدام تبدل ذواتها عند التركب، إذ التركب ليس إلا بمجاورته غيره، والشيء بمجاورته غيره لا يتبدل في نفسه بل يتبدل القدر، فكذا الأحكام المتعلقة بالذات لا تتبدل لأنها تابعة للذات تبقى ببقائها وتزول بزوالها، ولما كانت الجواهر عند التركيب مرئية دل أنها عند التفرد مرئية وهي قائمة بالذات وليست بجسم، فكانت الرؤية دائرة مع القيام بالذات دون الجسم، فتتعدى إلى كل قائم بالذات، فمن أراد بالتعلق بهذه الطريقة ينبغي أن يسال الخصم أنه هل يرى رؤية الأعراض جائزة، فإن رآها مستحيلة الرؤية يكلمه على هذه الطريقة، وإن قال: إن بعض الأعراض مرئية فينبغي أن لا يتمسك بهذه الطريقة لأنه لا يمكنه بمشيئته إلا إذا رأي في استحالة إدراك الأعراض بالحواس كلها رأي عبد الله بن سعيد، فيتكلم بالمعقول الذي بينا في استحالة رؤية الأعراض وإدراكها بالحواس من وجود الخلاف فيها بين أرباب الحواس السليمة، حينئذ يمكنه الكلام، فأما غير عبد الله من قدماء أصحابنا فإنما كانوا يعلقون الرؤية بالقائم بالذات بمساعدة المعتزلة إياهم في القول باستحالة رؤية الأعراض لا غير. وكان الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي يرى الأصوب في هذه المسألة أن يتمسك بالدلائل السمعية التي مر ذكرها. فإذا عجزت الخصوم عن الاعتراض عليها وزعمت أن الدلائل السمعية إن أوجبت الرؤية فالدلائل العقلية منعت القول بالرؤية فكانت الآيات والأحاديث متشابهة يجب التوقف فيها. فيقال لهم : لم قلتم إن الدلائل العقلية منعت القول بالرؤية؟ فإذا قالوا: لأن المرئي هو الجسم بدليل أن في المشاهدة لا يرى إلا الجسم والله تعالى ليس بجسم يطالبون بدليل. وقيل لهم : لم قلتم إن الجسم يرى لأنه جسم؟ ويعارضون أنها تعلقت بالأجسام لا لكونها أجساما بل لكونها قائمة بالذات فيعلل بها على طريق المعارضة دفعا لكلام الخصوم لا لابتداء التعليل ليقع الدليل على الخصم فيهدم حينئذ ما يقيم من الدلالة على تعلق الرؤية بالجسم على ما بينا، ولا يحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل على تعلقها بالقائم بالذات، فكان هذا أضيق على الخصم وأحسم لمادة شغبه فإنه – رحمه الله – ذكر في المقالات في مسألة المائية فقال : ثم الأصل أن ما لا يرى في الشاهد عرض يقول بغيره وإلا فكل قائم بنفسه يرى فإذا كان الله تعالى قائما بذاته يرى لا على الاعتلال بذلك ولكن على الرفع والمقابلة إذ لم يرجع في الأمر إلى ما يوجبه الدليل لكن رجع إلى الوجود في الشاهد هذا لفظه - رحمه الله - ومعناه ما بينا. وكذا لو سلكت هذه الطريقة مع المتأخرين القائلين بجواز رؤية
Vision and its impossibility relate to the essence, and the essence does not change by composition, so the judgment pertaining to the essence does not change. This is because what changes by composition are the judgments pertaining to measures and quantities. For that which is not long, when it joins with that which is not long, they both become long; likewise the square, triangle, hexagon, and other shapes. As for the judgments pertaining to essences, there is no change for them by composition because of the absence of change of their essences at composition, since composition is nothing but adjacency to another thing, and a thing by adjacency to another does not change in itself but the quantity changes. Likewise, the judgments pertaining to the essence do not change because they are dependent on the essence, remaining with its remaining and ceasing with its ceasing. And since atoms at composition are visible, it indicates that they are visible at separation, and they are self-subsisting (qa'im bi al-dhat) and not a body. Thus vision revolves around self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-dhat) without the body, so it extends to every self-subsisting thing. Whoever wants to adhere to this method should ask the opponent: 'Do you consider the vision of accidents (a'rad) permissible?' If he considers them impossible to see, he engages him on this method. If he says: 'Some accidents are visible,' then he should not adhere to this method because he cannot; only if he adopts the view of 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id on the impossibility of perceiving accidents by all senses, then he may speak by the rational argument we explained on the impossibility of seeing and perceiving accidents by the senses, given the disagreement among people of sound senses. At that point he can speak. As for other than 'Abd Allah among our early companions, they only attached vision to the self-subsisting with the assistance of the Mu'tazila agreeing with them on the statement of the impossibility of seeing accidents, nothing else. And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi saw the soundest in this issue to adhere to the scriptural proofs (dala'il sam'iyya) mentioned earlier. If the opponents fail to object to them and claim that the scriptural proofs necessitate vision, but rational proofs (dala'il 'aqliyya) prevent affirming vision, so the verses and hadiths become ambiguous (mutashabihat) and must be paused upon, it is said to them: 'Why do you say that rational proofs prevent affirming vision?' If they say: 'Because the visible is the body, as evidenced by the fact that in witnessing nothing is seen except the body, and God is not a body,' they are asked for a proof. And it is said to them: 'Why do you say that the body is seen because it is a body?' And they are countered that vision attaches to bodies not because they are bodies but because they are self-subsisting. So it is reasoned by way of counterargument (mu'arada) to repel the opponents' speech, not for initiating reasoning, so that the proof falls on the opponent and then demolishes what he establishes of evidence for the attachment of vision to the body as we explained. There is no need to establish proof for its attachment to the self-subsisting. This is more constricting for the opponent and more decisive against the matter of his misgiving. For he – may God have mercy on him – mentioned in al-Maqalat in the issue of 'matter' (al-ma'iyya) and said: 'Then the principle is that what is not seen in the witnessed world is an accident that exists by another, otherwise every self-subsistent thing is seen. So since God is self-subsistent, He is seen, not by way of reasoning to that but by way of elevation and comparison, because the matter does not return to what the proof necessitates but returns to existence in the witnessed world.' This is his wording – may God have mercy on him – and its meaning is what we explained. And likewise if this method is followed with later scholars who affirm the permissibility of seeing...
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument for the visibility of God (ru'ya) and the preferred approach of Imam al-Maturidi. The first part of the passage from page 613 argues that visibility is attached to being self-subsisting (qa'im bi al-dhat) rather than to being a body. The author begins by stating that vision or its impossibility relates to the essence (dhat), and since essence does not change by composition (tarkib), the judgment pertaining to the essence does not change. He explains that composition only changes judgments related to quantities and measures, not to essences. For example, two short items together become long, but each individual item's essence does not change. Similarly, judgments pertaining to essence are dependent on it and remain as long as it remains. He then applies this to atoms (jawahir): when atoms are composed, they are visible; therefore, when separate, they are also visible because they are self-subsisting and not bodies. Thus, vision revolves around self-subsistence, not body. This argument extends to every self-subsisting entity, including God. The author then discusses how to debate an opponent (presumably the Mu'tazila who denied seeing God). If the opponent denies seeing accidents, the argument works; if they affirm seeing some accidents, the debater must first establish the impossibility of perceiving accidents by the senses, as 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id held. Otherwise, the argument relies on the Mu'tazila's agreement that accidents cannot be seen. The second part, beginning on page 614, presents al-Maturidi's preferred method: to rely on scriptural proofs (dala'il sam'iyya). When opponents claim that rational proofs prevent vision, they are asked why. If they say the visible is only the body, they are asked for proof. Then a counterargument (mu'arada) is used: bodies are seen not because they are bodies but because they are self-subsisting. This counterargument is not a positive proof but a refutation of the opponent's claim. Al-Maturidi quotes his own work al-Maqalat, stating that the principle is: whatever is not seen in this world is an accident, otherwise every self-subsistent thing is seen. Therefore, God, being self-subsistent, is seen, not by applying a causal principle but by analogy with the witnessed world. The passage ends with a note about applying this method to later scholars who affirm the permissibility of seeing (possibly God), though the text is cut off.

Key Terms
دلائل سمعية
دلائل عقلية
معارضة
قائم بالذات
رؤية
متشابهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational proofs (dala'il 'aqliyya) conclusively prove that God cannot be seen.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that the rational proofs are not decisive and can be countered by mu'arada; thus, scriptural proofs remain valid.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, what is the proper response when opponents claim that rational proofs prevent vision?
A) Accept the rational proofs and abandon scriptural proofs.
B) Use counterargument that bodies are seen because they are self-subsisting, not because they are bodies.
C) Reject both rational and scriptural proofs and rely on personal opinion.
D) Claim that rational proofs are irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi prioritizes scriptural proofs and uses counterargument to neutralize rational objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a rational doubt that seems to contradict a clear scriptural teaching, does your heart first waver or does it demand proof from the doubter? Which response is closer to the one al-Maturidi models here?
?
Al-Maturidi uses a counterargument that does not prove the vision of God by itself but only shows that the opponent's objection is not valid. Are you comfortable with faith that removes obstacles without providing full positive proof? What does that say about your trust in revelation?
?
The principle from the witnessed world is used cautiously as a refutation, not as a proof. Is there a risk that you use analogies from creation to defend God's attributes in a way that might inadvertently liken Him to creation? How can you follow al-Maturidi's approach of using such reasoning only to counter objections, not to define God?

Existence as the Cause of Visibility: Rational Proof for Seeing God
Proof that existence is the cause of visibility, applicable to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how later Maturidi/Ash'ari scholars prove that existence alone is the cause making vision possible, and how this applies to God.

Core Idea
Existence, not physical attributes, is the sole cause of visibility.

This passage presents the rational argument used by later Maturidi and Ash'ari theologians for the visibility of God. They identify existence as the common factor in all visible things, and by consensus of their opponents, some accidents are visible, proving that existence is the sufficient cause. This lesson unpacks the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence; the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the absolute cause
Locate the statement that existence is the absolute cause (العلة المطلقة) of vision in the perceptible world. Mark it as the central claim.
3
Examine the proof from empirical observation
The passage lists three categories of visible things: substances, colors, and accidents. Note how each is observed and used as evidence.
The consensus of Mu'tazila scholars on the visibility of some accidents strengthens the argument.
4
Understand the additional proof from discrimination
The passage mentions that we distinguish black from white and conjoined from separate by sight, without these attributes subsisting in the eye. This shows accidents are directly visible.
5
Connect to God
Since existence is the cause and God exists, He too is rationally visible. Reflect on why this argument avoids the need for God to have a body or color.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن المتأخرين من أصحابنا عند دعوى المتأخرين من المعتزلة رؤية بعض الأعراض – ذهبوا إلى أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية في الشاهد المجوزة لها، الوجود. وأن كل موجود رؤيته ممكنة جائزة ودليل ذلك أنا نرى الجوهر والألوان والأكوان وهي الحركة والسكون والقرب والبعد والاجتماع والافتراق، وقد وافقنا الجبائي، على أن هذه الأجناس الثلاثة مرئية وابنه أبو هاشم وافقنا على رؤية الألوان، وخالفنا في رؤية الأكوان والنظام وافقنا على رؤية الألوان غير أنها أجسام، ولقد أقمنا الدلائل على كونها أعراض، فإجماع هؤلاء أغنانا عن إقامة الدليل على رؤية بعض الأعراض، وكونها جائزة الرؤية ثم الدليل مع هذا على رؤية الأعراض التمييز بالبصر بين الأسود والأبيض وبين المجتمعين والمفترقين من غير قيام السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق بآلة الرؤية وهو البصر، ولم
That the later ones from our companions, when the later ones from the Mu'tazila claimed the visibility of some accidents – went to the view that the absolute cause of vision in the perceptible world that makes it possible is existence. And that every existent thing's visibility is possible and permissible. And the proof of that is that we see substance, colors, and accidents such as movement and rest, proximity and distance, conjunction and separation. And al-Jubba'i agreed with us that these three kinds are visible; and his son Abu Hashim agreed with us on the visibility of colors but disagreed with us on the visibility of accidents; and al-Nazzam agreed with us on the visibility of colors but held they are bodies. And we have established proofs that they are accidents. So the consensus of these people sufficed us from establishing proof for the visibility of some accidents, and that their visibility is permissible. Then the proof for the visibility of accidents is the discrimination by sight between black and white, and between the conjoined and separate, while blackness, whiteness, conjunction, and separation do not subsist in the instrument of vision, which is the eye. And not...
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof used by later Maturidi and Ash'ari scholars for the visibility of God. They argue that the absolute cause of vision in the physical world is existence (al-wujud). Anything that exists can potentially be seen. The proof is that we empirically see entities belonging to three categories: substances (al-jawhar), colors (al-alwan), and accidents (al-akwan) like motion, rest, proximity, etc. The Mu'tazila leaders al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and al-Nazzam agreed on the visibility of at least some of these, especially colors. Their consensus suffices to establish that some accidents are visible. Additional evidence is that we distinguish black from white and conjoined from separate through sight, which proves that accidents are perceived without them subsisting in the eye itself. Since existence is the common factor among all these visible things, it must be the cause. And since God exists, He too is visible.

Key Terms
العلة المطلقة
الوجود
الرؤية
الجوهر
العرض
الأكوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical attributes like color or being a body are necessary for visibility.
Correction: This proof shows that existence alone is the cause, so God can be seen without embodied attributes.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what is the absolute cause of visibility in the physical world?
A) Substance
B) Color
C) Existence
D) Being an accident

Key Takeaway
Existence is the universal cause of visibility, and because God exists, it is rationally possible to see Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you truly believed that you will see Allah on the Day of Judgment, how would your longing for that vision change your priorities today? Are you preparing your heart for that meeting, or are you letting the seen world consume you?
?
The proof here relies on rational consensus among theologians. Where does your certainty about the possibility of seeing Allah come from — is it from such reasoning, or from the Qur'an and Sunnah? If someone challenged you with a rational objection, could you defend your belief with clear argument, or would you falter?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment and being granted the vision of His face. Would you be overjoyed, or would shame from your negligence overwhelm you? What specific sin or distraction in your life today would you need to abandon to truly hope for that vision without regret?

Why Not Seeing Movement Doesn't Prove Invisibility
Nasafi answers the objection about ship movement using analogy of colors on a spinning object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that ship movement is not seen, using analogies to show that failure to perceive something does not prove it is invisible.

Core Idea
Perception depends on conditions; invisibility does not follow from not perceiving.

A key objection against the visibility of accidents is the ship rider who does not see the ship's movement. Abu Hashim argued that if movement were visible, the rider would see it. Nasafi responds by comparing this to other cases where perception fails but the object remains visible in principle. This lesson unpacks his analogies and the underlying logic that saves the argument from the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full argument. Notice the structure: objection, then answer with analogies, then a final point about existence.
The objection is from Abu Hashim, a Mu'tazili scholar.
2
Analyze the first analogy: Sophists and mirage
Nasafi compares the objection to the Sophist denial of seeing bodies because of mirage. Just because a mirage is seen from afar but not up close does not mean bodies are invisible. Similarly, not seeing ship movement from the ship does not mean movement is invisible.
This shows that lack of perception under certain conditions does not negate visibility in general.
3
Analyze the second analogy: colored arrow and spindle
Next, Nasafi gives the example of an arrow or spindle with multiple colors that appears as one color when moving. The other colors exist but are not perceived. This strengthens the point that failure to perceive does not disprove existence or visibility.
The analogy is 'rather more so' because even an external observer cannot distinguish the colors at that moment.
4
Understand the rider's own perception
Nasafi adds that if the rider looks at the water, he does see the ship's movement. So even the rider can perceive movement under the right conditions. This refutes the claim that the rider never sees it.
5
Grasp the final argument: existence as basis
Finally, Nasafi returns to the earlier proof that visibility is based on existence (wujūd), which is common to both colors and substances. Since colors are visibly existent, accidents are too. The objection does not undermine this foundation.
This is the logical core: the attribute that makes something visible is existence, not accidental qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لا وجه إلى ادعاء رؤية الأكوان فإن راكب السفينة لا يرى تحركها ولا يحس ذلك، ولو كانت مرئية لرآها الراكب، وهو شبهة أبي هاشم. قلنا: يمثل هذه الشبهة تنكر السوفسطائية رؤية الأجسام، فإنهم يزعمون أن من البر يرى السراب وغيره لا يرى ممن كان يقرب من السراب ولم يوجب ذلك قدحا في رؤية الأجسام، فكذا ما ألزمت على أن اللون عندك مرئي، ولو أن سهما صبغ بأصباغ مختلفة ثم رمي، أو فلكة مغزل أو عتبة تصبغ بأصباغ مختلفة ثم تدار لا يرى إلا لون واحد، وما وراء هذا الواحد لا يرى وإن كان في حال رمى السهم ودوران المغزل موجودا فكذا حركة السفينة بل أولى لأن تلك الألوان لا يقف عليها ولا يعرفها في تلك الحالة أحد من الناس، وحركة السفينة يقف عليها غير راكبها وكذا راكبها لو نظر إلى الماء رأي حركة السفينة، ثم ذلك لم يوجب استحالة رؤية الألوان فكذا ما ألزمت على أن الأكوان وإن لم يتعلق بها الرؤية في الشاهد لا يبطل ذلك اعتلالنا بالوجود، لأن الألوان مرئية بلا خلاف بيننا وبين أبي هاشم، ولا وصف يجمع الألوان والجواهر يمكن تعلق الرؤية به إلا الوجود على ما سبق تقديره، فإذا هذا السؤال لا يجدي التعلق به نفعا للخصوم.
They said: There is no way to claim the visibility of accidents (al-akwān), for the rider of a ship does not see its movement nor perceive it, and if they were visible the rider would see them, and this is the doubt (shubha) of Abu Hashim. We said: This doubt is matched by the Sophists' denial of the visibility of bodies, for they claim that one on land sees the mirage and something else which is not seen by one who is near the mirage, and that did not cause a flaw in the visibility of bodies. Similarly, what you have required, that color is visible to you, and if an arrow is dyed with different colors and then shot, or a spindle whorl or a threshold is dyed with different colors and then spun, only one color is seen, and what is beyond this one is not seen, although at the moment of shooting the arrow and spinning the spindle it exists, so too with the movement of the ship, rather more so, because those colors are not known nor recognized by anyone in that state, and the movement of the ship is known by someone other than its rider, and likewise its rider, if he looks at the water, sees the movement of the ship. Then that did not necessitate the impossibility of seeing colors, so too what you have required, that accidents, even if sight does not relate to them in the empirical world, does not invalidate our argument by existence, because colors are visible without disagreement between us and Abu Hashim, and there is no attribute that combines colors and substances (jawāhir) that can relate to sight except existence (al-wujūd), as previously established. So this question, by adhering to it, does not benefit the opponents.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent (Abu Hashim) raises a doubt: if ship movement were visible, the rider would see it, but he does not, therefore movement is not visible. Nasafi responds by comparing this to the Sophist denial of seeing bodies: just because the mirage is seen from afar but not up close does not disprove that bodies are visible. Similarly, an arrow or spindle dyed with multiple colors appears as only one color when moving, but that does not mean the other colors are invisible in principle—they are not perceived due to the speed of movement. In the same way, the rider does not perceive the ship's movement because he is moving with it, but if he looks at the water he does see the movement. Furthermore, the argument for visibility of accidents is based on existence (wujūd), which is common to both colors and substances. Since colors are indisputably visible, and the attribute enabling visibility is existence, accidents (akwān) are also visible by virtue of existence. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
akwān (accidents)
ru'ya (vision)
shubha (doubt)
sufsatā'iyya (Sophists)
lawn (color)
wujūd (existence)
jawāhir (substances)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is not perceived under certain conditions, it must be invisible or nonexistent.
Correction: Perception depends on conditions (e.g., speed, position). Failure to perceive does not negate the existence or visibility of the object in principle.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Nasafi use to refute the claim that the movement of a ship is not seen?
A) The sun and moon
B) An arrow or spindle dyed with multiple colors
C) A mirage
D) Sound

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that failure to perceive something does not prove its invisibility, and the visibility of accidents is grounded in existence, not immediate perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged a truth claim to be false simply because you could not perceive it directly? When? How will you change your approach after learning that non-perception does not prove invisibility?
?
The rider on the ship is too close to see its movement, yet it is moving. In your spiritual life, are you so immersed in your routines that you fail to perceive the hand of God moving around you? What one concrete step will you take today to 'look at the water' and see His signs?
?
Nasafi used analogies to correct a mistake in reasoning. What is one area of your faith where you hold a doubt because you haven't examined the conditions of your perception? Will you commit to studying that doubt further with an open heart?

The Objection from Exclusive Sense Perception
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite objection that visibility would imply color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that if God were visible, He would have to be a color, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Visibility exclusive to sight would make God like a color.

The Mu'tazila argued that in the empirical world, only color is perceived exclusively by sight. Since God is not perceived by other senses, if He were seen, He would share this exclusive property with color, implying He is a color—an impossibility for the transcendent Creator. This lesson presents this objection in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full force of the opponent's argument.
2
Identify the empirical premise
Notice how the opponent distinguishes between substances/accidents (perceptible by touch) and color (perceptible only by sight).
This distinction is key to the argument.
3
See the application to God
Observe how the opponent reasons that if God were seen, His perception would be exclusive to sight, making Him analogous to color, which is impossible.
The conclusion is that God cannot be seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا رؤية الجواهر والأكوان والألوان وإن ثبتت في الشاهد غير أن ما وراء الألوان وهو الجواهر والأكوان ليس بمختص بالوقوف عليه بالرؤية بل قد يمكن إدراك ذلك بما وراء البصر وهي المس، فإن الأعمى يعرف وجود الجسم وحركته وسكونه واجتماع الجسمين وافتراقهما بالمس كما يعرف ذلك بالبصر فأما اللون فهو لا يوقف عليه إلا بحاسة البصر، وبهذا لا يعرف الأعمى لون الجسم بالمس فكان اللون هو المختص بالإدراك بحاسة البصر على معنى أنه لا يدرك بشيء من الحواس إلا بالبصر، ولو كان الباري جل وعلا مرئيا مع أنه ليس بمسموع ولا بممسوس ولا مذوق ولا مشموم لكان إدراكه مختصا بالرؤية فكان لونا إذ هذا من خاصة اللون، وحيث يستحيل أن يكون الله تعالى من جنس الألوان، استحالة أن يكون مختصا بالإدراك بالبصر. فبعد ذلك إما أن يقال : إنه مسموع كما أنه مبصر أو مذوق أو مشموم وذا محال وإما أن يمتنع عليه الرؤية.
They said: The visibility of substances (jawāhir) and accidents (akwān) and colors is established in the empirical world, but that which is beyond colors, namely substances and accidents, is not specific to being known by sight, but rather it can be perceived by something other than sight, which is touch. For the blind person knows the existence of a body, its motion and rest, the conjunction of two bodies and their separation by touch, just as he knows that by sight. As for color, it is not known except by the sense of sight, and by this the blind person does not know the color of a body by touch. Thus color is specific to perception by the sense of sight, meaning that it is not perceived by any of the senses except by sight. And if the Exalted Creator (al-Bāri') were visible while He is not audible, nor tangible, nor tasteable, nor smellable, then His perception would be specific to sight, so He would be a color, for this is a special property of color. And since it is impossible that God most high is of the genus of colors, it is impossible that He is specific to perception by sight. After that, either it is said that He is audible just as He is visible, or tasteable or smellable, and that is impossible, or else it is prevented that He is seen.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponents (likely Mu'tazila) against the visibility of God. The opponent argues: in the empirical world, substances and accidents can be perceived by both sight and touch, but color is unique in that it is perceived only by sight. If God were visible, He would have to be perceived solely by sight (since He is not perceived by other senses), which is a property specific to color. Therefore, God would be like a color, which is impossible because God is not a body with color. Thus, it is impossible for God to be seen. The opponent then presents a dilemma: either God would also have to be perceived by other senses (audible, tasteable, etc.)—which is absurd—or else He cannot be seen at all. This objection aims to prove that God's visibility is impossible. The author likely responds in subsequent pages, but within this passage the objection is presented in full.

Key Terms
jawāhir (substances)
akwān (accidents)
lawn (color)
bāri' (Creator)
ru'ya (vision)
hiss al-basar (sense of sight)
mass (touch)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visibility does not automatically entail being a color; the objection assumes a false analogy.
Correction: The objection does not claim that visibility equals being a color, but that exclusive perception by sight is a property specific to color in the empirical world. If God were seen, He would share that exclusive property, making Him analogous to color. This is a logical argument, not an assertion of identity.

Checkpoint
Why does the opponent argue that if God were visible, He would have to be like color?
A) Because color is the only thing perceived exclusively by sight
B) Because both are eternal
C) Because both are perceived by touch
D) Because color and bodies are both created

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection uses the unique perception of color to argue that divine visibility would reduce God to a bodily attribute, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson challenges you to accept that God transcends all sensory experience. Are you willing to let go of your mental images of God and submit to the truths revealed about Him, even when they defy your senses? Commit to one concrete way you will practice this submission today.
?
The blind man knows a body by touch but not its color. You have not seen God, but you 'touch' Him through His signs and actions. How deeply have you engaged with these signs? Are you content with indirect knowledge, or do you yearn for the vision of God in the Hereafter? What does this yearning demand of you now in terms of sincerity and obedience? Resolve to seek Him through His signs with greater attentiveness starting this moment.
?
The Mu'tazila demanded that God conform to their empirical logic, a logic that would make Him like a color. Where in your life have you insisted that God operate according to your own limited reasoning? Identify one area where you have doubted or limited His power because you could not see or understand it. Confess that limitation and trust that He is beyond your senses, yet near. Take one step of action that relies solely on faith in His unseen reality.

Vision Attaches to Existence, Not Color
Nasafi refutes an objection about akwan by arguing that existence is the common attribute for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that existence (wujud) is the common attribute that makes both colors and atoms visible, and how he refutes the objection that accidents are not seen.

Core Idea
Vision attaches to existence, not to color or accidents.

This passage addresses a key objection in the debate about the possibility of seeing God. The opponents argue that since we do not see accidents like motion, vision cannot attach to existence alone. Nasafi counters with a compelling analogy and a clear logical principle: the only attribute common to both colors and atoms is existence, so vision must attach to existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the flow: an analogy followed by a logical conclusion.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponents claimed that accidents (akwan) are not visible, so vision cannot attach to existence. Find this in the passage.
The phrase 'akwan' refers to accidents like motion and rest.
3
Examine Nasafi's analogy
Nasafi uses the example of a ship's movement: a rider sees it differently than an observer, yet both can see colors. This shows that not perceiving some things does not negate the principle.
4
Grasp the core argument
Nasafi asserts that the only attribute common to both colors and atoms is existence. Therefore, vision must attach to existence itself.
This is the key: existence is the necessary condition for visibility.
5
Conclude why the objection fails
Since the opponents cannot point to another common attribute, their objection does not weaken Nasafi's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليها ولا يعرفها في تلك الحالة أحد من الناس ، وحركة السفينة يقف عليها غير راكبها وكذا راكبها لو نظر إلى الماء رأى حركة السفينة، ثم ذلك لم يوجب استحالة رؤية الألوان فكذا ما ألزمت على أن الأكوان وإن لم يتعلق بها الرؤية في الشاهد لا يبطل ذلك اعتلالنا بالوجود، لأن الألوان مرئية بلا خلاف بيننا وبين أبي هاشم، ولا وصف يجمع الألوان والجواهر يمكن تعلق الرؤية به إلا الوجود على ما سبق تقديره، فإذا هذا السؤال لا يجدي التعلق به نفعا للخصوم.
That and it is not known by anyone in that state. The movement of the ship is known by its non-rider, and likewise its rider if he looks at the water sees the movement of the ship. Then that did not necessitate the impossibility of seeing colors. So likewise what you objected concerning that accidents (akwan) even if vision does not attach to them in the witnessed world, does not invalidate our argument based on existence (wujud), because colors are visible without disagreement between us and Abu Hashim, and there is no attribute that gathers colors and atoms (jawahir) to which vision can attach except existence, as previously established. So this question does not yield benefit by clinging to it for the opponents.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is part of a broader argument about the possibility of seeing God. The author responds to an objection by first giving an analogy: the movement of a ship is perceived differently by a rider and an observer, yet both can see colors. He then argues that even if accidents (akwan) — such as motion and rest — are not directly seen, that does not refute his claim that existence (wujud) is the common attribute enabling vision. Since colors are unquestionably visible, and the only attribute shared between colors and atoms is existence, the opponents' objection fails.

Correction: Nasafi clarifies that vision attaches to existence itself, not to color or accidents.

Nasafi's Refutation of the Color Objection
Nasafi refutes the objection that visibility implies color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that God cannot be seen because visibility would imply color.

Core Idea
Visibility does not require color; even color can be heard according to al-Ash'ari.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a common objection against the vision of God: that only colors are perceived exclusively by sight, so if God were visible He would have to be a color. He responds by showing that the objection relies on ignorance of both the opponents' own doctrines and the factual nature of perception. Journey with him as he dismantles the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponents present an analogy from the witnessed world: only color is exclusive to sight; other things can be touched. Since God is not tangible, He would have to be a color, which is impossible.
2
Identify Nasafi's first counter
Nasafi accuses the opponents of ignorance of the doctrines of 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id and al-Ash'ari, who held that God can be seen and heard, and that color itself can be heard, breaking the exclusivity.
3
Examine the second counter
Nasafi challenges the factual claim that motion and rest are perceived by touch in the same way as sight, questioning whether they are 'touched' or merely known through touch.
The passage cuts off here, but the argument continues.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Connect both counters to see how Nasafi undermines the analogy: the premises are false or misapplied, so the conclusion that God cannot be seen does not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا رؤية الجواهر والأكوان والألوان وإن ثبتت في الشاهد غير أن ما وراء الألوان وهو الجواهر والأكوان ليس بمختص بالوقوف عليه بالرؤية بل قد يمكن إدراك ذلك بما وراء البصر وهي المس، فإن الأعمى يعرف وجود الجسم وحركته وسكونه واجتماع الجسمين وافتراقهما بالمس كما يعرف ذلك بالبصر فأما اللون فهو لا يوقف عليه إلا بحاسة البصر، وبهذا لا يعرف الأعمى لون الجسم بالمس فكان اللون هو المختص بالإدراك بحاسة البصر على معنى أنه لا يدرك بشيء من الحواس إلا بالبصر، ولو كان الباري جل وعلا مرئيا مع أنه ليس بمسموع ولا بممسوس ولا مذوق ولا مشموم لكان إدراكه مختصا بالرؤية فكان لونا إذ هذا من خاصة اللون، وحيث يستحيل أن يكون الله تعالى من جنس الألوان، استحالة أن يكون مختصا بالإدراك بالبصر. فبعد ذلك إما أن يقال : إنه مسموع كما أنه مبصر أو مذوق أو مشموم وذا محال وإما أن يمتنع عليه الرؤية. قيل لهم: هذا كله تمويه أو جهل بمذاهب الخصوم وبالحقائق. أما الجهل بمذاهب الخصوم فإن عبد الله بن سعيد وأبا الحسن الأشعري كانا يقولان: إن ذات الباري تعالى يجوز أن [؟] كما يجوز أن يرى، فلم يكن عذرهما الذات مختصا يسمع [؟] بالرؤية. فبطل هذا الكلام. ثم إن عند الأشعري اللون يجوز أن يسمع أيضا فلم يكن للون اختصاص بالرؤية ولا لذات الباري جل وعلا. ثم استواء ذات الباري واللون في أن كل واحد منهما يجوز أن يسمع ويرى لا يوجب أن يكون الباري جل وعلا لونا، لأن كل واحد منهما أعني الرؤية والسمع يتعلق بكل موجود، فيتعلق بالمتضادات والمختلفات ولا وجب التشابه والاستواء بينهما فكذا هذا. وأما الجهل بالحقائق فلأنهم زعموا أن اللون لا يدرك إلا بالبصر والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق تدرك بالمس كما تدرك بالبصر، وهذا جهل منهم. وذلك أنا نقول لهم : أيش تعنون بقولكم : إن اللون لا يدرك إلا بالبصر؟ أتعنون أنه لا يعلم البتة إلا بالحس ويرى ليعلم ولا طريق للعلم به سواه؟ ثم يقال لهم: أيش تعنون بقولكم : إن الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق يدرك بالمس؟ أتعنون أنها تمس فتكون ممسوسة كما تكون مرئية أو أنها عند مس الجسم تعلم من غير أن
If they say: The vision of atoms (jawahir), accidents (akwan), and colors is established in the witnessed world, but what is beyond colors — namely atoms and accidents — is not specific to being known by vision, rather they can be perceived by what is beyond sight, which is touch (mass). For the blind person knows the existence of the body, its movement and rest, the joining and separation of two bodies by touch just as he knows that by sight. As for color, it is not known except by the sense of sight, and by this the blind person does not know the color of the body by touch. So color is specific to perception by the sense of sight in the sense that it is not perceived by any of the senses except by sight. If the Exalted and Majestic Creator were visible while He is not audible, nor tangible, nor tasteable, nor smellable, then His perception would be specific to vision and He would be a color, for this is a property of color. And since it is impossible for God Almighty to be of the genus of colors, it is impossible for Him to be specific to perception by sight. After that, either it should be said that He is audible just as He is visible, or tasteable, or smellable — and that is impossible — or else vision of Him must be impossible. It is said to them: All this is obfuscation or ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents and of the facts. As for the ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents: 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari used to say that the essence of the Exalted Creator may be [?] just as it may be seen, so the excuse of those two was not that the essence is specific to being heard [?] by vision. So this speech is invalid. Then, according to al-Ash'ari, color may also be heard, so color does not have exclusivity to vision, nor does the essence of the Exalted and Majestic Creator. Then the equality of the essence of the Creator and color in that each of them may be heard and seen does not necessitate that the Exalted and Majestic Creator be a color, because each of them—I mean vision and hearing—attaches to every existent, so it attaches to opposites and different things, and sameness and equality between them are not necessitated. So likewise this. As for ignorance of the facts: they claimed that color is not perceived except by sight, while movement, rest, joining, and separation are perceived by touch just as they are perceived by sight—this is ignorance on their part. For we say to them: What do you mean by your statement that color is not perceived except by sight? Do you mean that it is not known at all except by sense and is seen to be known, and there is no way to know it other than that? Then we say to them: What do you mean by your statement that movement, rest, joining, and separation are perceived by touch? Do you mean that they are touched and thus are tangible just as they are visible, or that when the body is touched they are known without...
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents argue: In the witnessed world, only color is perceived exclusively by sight; other things like atoms and accidents can also be perceived by touch. If God were visible but not perceived by other senses, He would have to be a color, which is impossible. Therefore, God cannot be seen. Nasafi responds by first accusing the opponents of ignorance of the doctrines of key figures like 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who held that God can be both seen and heard, so the essence is not unique to vision. He also notes that al-Ash'ari held that color itself can be heard, so color is not exclusive to sight. Moreover, equality in being seen and heard does not imply that God becomes a color. Then he attacks the factual claim that motion and rest are perceived by touch, arguing that the opponents conflate knowing through touch with touching itself. The passage ends mid-argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is visible, it must be a color.
Correction: Visibility does not require color; even color can be heard according to al-Ash'ari, so the exclusive link between sight and color is broken.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Nasafi on the Refutation of Ignorance of Opponents' Doctrines
Nasafi refutes the claim of ignorance regarding Ibn Sa'id and Ash'ari on God's visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that opponents are ignorant of the doctrines of Ibn Sa'id and Ash'ari on God's visibility, and why being seen or heard does not imply corporeality.

Core Idea
God's essence can be seen and heard without becoming a color or body.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla addresses a key objection against the Ash'ari position on the visibility of God. The opponents had argued that if God is visible, He must be a body or color. Nasafi counters by showing that both God's essence and color can be heard and seen, yet this does not make God a color. Follow along as he dismantles the claim of ignorance regarding the doctrines of Ibn Sa'id and al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to how Nasafi structures his refutation.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents claimed that God's essence is heard but not seen. Note how Nasafi refutes this by stating the actual position of Ibn Sa'id and Ash'ari.
3
Understand Ash'ari's view on color
Nasafi adds that Ash'ari held color can also be heard, so color is not exclusive to sight. This shows that the opponents' reasoning is flawed.
This step strengthens the refutation.
4
Analyze the logical analogy
Nasafi argues that equality in being heard and seen does not necessitate identity in essence. Sight and hearing relate to all existents, including opposites.
This is the core of the argument against anthropomorphism.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Conclude that God's essence is not a color or body, and the opponents' claim of ignorance is baseless.
The lesson reinforces divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم: هذا كله تمويه أو جهل بمذاهب الخصوم وبالحقائق. أما الجهل بمذاهب الخصوم فإن عبد الله بن سعيد وأبا الحسن الأشعري كانا يقولان: إن ذات الباري تعالى يجوز أن كما يجوز أن يرى، فلم يكن عذرهما الذات مختصا يسمع بالرؤية. فبطل هذا الكلام. ثم إن عند الأشعري اللون يجوز أن يسمع أيضا فلم يكن للون اختصاص بالرؤية ولا لذات الباري جل وعلا. ثم استواء ذات الباري واللون في أن كل واحد منهما يجوز أن يسمع ويرى لا يوجب أن يكون الباري جل وعلا لونا، لأن كل واحد منهما أعني الرؤية والسمع يتعلق بكل موجود، فيتعلق بالمتضادات والمختلفات ولا وجب التشابه والاستواء بينهما، فكذا هذا.
It was said to them: All this is either obfuscation or ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents and of the realities. As for ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents: 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari used to say that the essence of the Exalted Creator may be heard just as it may be seen. So their excuse that the essence is specifically heard without sight is invalid. Thus this speech is false. Then, according to al-Ash'ari, color may also be heard, so color is not specific to sight, nor is the essence of the Exalted and Majestic. Then the equality of the essence of the Exalted and color in that each of them may be heard and seen does not necessitate that the Exalted and Majestic be a color, because each of them—that is, sight and hearing—relates to every existent, so it relates to opposites and differences, and this does not necessitate likeness and equality between them. So it is with this.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the opponents are ignorant of the doctrines of their adversaries and of the realities. Nasafi first addresses the first part: ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents. He states that Ibn Sa'id and al-Ash'ari held that God's essence can be heard just as it can be seen. Thus, the opponents' claim that God's essence is specifically heard (not seen) is false. Nasafi then adds that according to al-Ash'ari, even color can be heard, so color is not exclusively visible. Therefore, the fact that both God's essence and color can be both seen and heard does not imply that God is a color. The reason is that sight and hearing relate to every existent, including opposites and different things, and this does not necessitate similarity between them. The argument demonstrates that the opponents' reasoning is flawed because it assumes that if two things share a property, they must be the same kind, which is false.

تشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God can be seen, He must be a color or a body.
Correction: Nasafi shows that visibility does not entail being a color, as both God and color can be seen and heard, yet they are fundamentally different.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the main error in the opponents' claim about God's essence being heard but not seen?

B) They think color can be heard.
C) They confuse sight and hearing.
D) They deny God's attributes.

Key Takeaway
God's essence can be seen and heard without being a color or body, as the senses relate to all existents without implying similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you will see God in the afterlife without reducing Him to a physical form? What in your heart resists this belief, and how will you combat that doubt starting today?
?
The passage argues that sharing a property with something does not make them the same. Are you guilty of assuming that God's attributes make Him similar to creation? How will you purify your tawhid from such assumptions this week?
?
If you were challenged to defend the Ash'ari view on seeing God, would you be able to present this argument clearly? What step will you take to ensure you can, especially when speaking to someone who accuses you of anthropomorphism?

Refuting the Tangible Perception of Motion
Nasafi refutes the claim that motion is perceived by touch as a tangible accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi refutes the claim that motion, rest, conjunction, and separation are perceived by touch as tangible accidents, and that they are actually known through reason with the mediation of the senses.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be touched; only bodies can be tangible.

Nasafi begins by exposing the ignorance of his opponents who claim that motion and similar accidents are perceived by touch just as they are by sight. He interrogates their claim by asking whether they mean these accidents become tangible or are merely known through touch. This passage lays the groundwork for a crucial distinction between direct sensation and rational inference.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' claim
Notice that the opponents assert that color is only perceived by sight, while motion, rest, conjunction, and separation are perceived by both touch and sight. This is the claim Nasafi will refute.
The opponents are likely a sect that denies the visibility of accidents.
2
Analyze Nasafi's question about 'perceived by touch'
Nasafi asks whether they mean these accidents become tangible (like a body) or are known through touch without being tangible. This distinction is key.
Think about whether an accident like motion can have the property of tangibility.
3
Follow the refutation
If they mean tangible, Nasafi says this is impossible because tangence only subsists in bodies, not in accidents. If they mean known through touch without being tangible, then Nasafi argues that these accidents are not directly sensed but are known by reason through the sense of touch.
This shows the role of reason in perceiving motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجهل بالحقائق فلأنهم زعموا أن اللون لا يدرك إلا بالبصر والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق تدرك بالمس كما تدرك بالبصر، وهذا جهل منهم. وذلك أنا نقول لهم: أيش تعنون بقولكم: إن اللون لا يدرك إلا بالبصر؟ أتعنون أنه لا يعلم البتة إلا بالحس ويرى ليعلم ولا طريق للعلم به سواه؟ ثم يقال لهم: أيش تعنون بقولكم: إن الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق يدرك بالمس؟ أتعنون أنها تمس فتكون ممسوسة (كما تكون مرئية) أو أنها عند مس الجسم تعلم من غير أن تكون ممسوسة؟ فإن عنيتم أنها تمس فتكون ممسوسة كما تكون مرئية فهذا باطل إذ غير الجسم لا يمس، لاستحالة قيام المماسة بالأعراض، ولابد من المس من قيام المماسة بكل واحد من المتماسين. فإن قالوا: لا تصير ممسوسة ولكنها تصير معلومة بالحس بمس الجسم الذي هو محلها. قلنا ما لا يدخل تحت الحس لا يصير معلوما بالحس بل يصير معلوما بالعقل بواسطة الحس فكان العلم الحاصل بالاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون بالعقل بواسطة الحس إذ لم تصر هذه الأشياء ممسوسة كالعلم بالحرارة والبرودة والملامسة والخشونة والصلابة والرخاوة، وعند الرؤية تصير هذه الأشياء أعني الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق مرئية على ما قررنا. ثم بالوقوف على هذه الجملة الحقيقية. يقال لهم: إن عنيتم أن اللون لا يدرك إلا بالبصر ولا يحس بشيء من الحواس الأخر، فكذا الحكم في الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق فإذا لا اختصاص للون. وإن عنيتم أنه لا طريقة لمعرفته إلا الرؤية والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق لمعرفتها طريق آخر سوى الرؤية، قلنا: ذات الله تعالى تعرف بالعقل بواسطة مشاهدة الآيات الدالة عليه، فإن لم يجب تكون الأكوان وهي الحركة والسكون مرئية أن تكون لونا لما أنها تعرف بطريق آخر في الجملة في الحس، فلا يجب كون الباري جل وعلا لونا وإن كان مرئيا لأنه يعلم بطريق آخر فإن وجب وكونه تعالى لونا لكونه مرئيا، وإن كان لمعرفته طريق آخر سوى الرؤية، لوجب كون الأكوان ألوانا لكونها مرئية وإن كان لمعرفتها طريق آخر، والقول به خروج عن المعارف، فكذا هذا.
As for ignorance of the realities: because they claim that color is only perceived by sight, while motion, rest, conjunction, and separation are perceived by touch just as they are perceived by sight. This is ignorance on their part. For we say to them: What do you mean by your statement 'Color is only perceived by sight'? Do you mean that it is not known at all except by sensation and seen to be known, with no way of knowing it other than that? Then they are asked: What do you mean by your statement 'Motion, rest, conjunction, and separation are perceived by touch'? Do you mean that they are touched and become tangible (as they are visible), or that upon touching the body they are known without being tangible? If you mean that they are touched and become tangible as they are visible, this is false, because nothing other than a body can be touched, due to the impossibility of touch subsisting in accidents, and touch necessarily requires the subsistence of tangence in each of the two touching things. If they say: They do not become tangible, but they become known by sensation through touching the body that is their locus. We say: That which does not fall under sensation does not become known by sensation, but rather becomes known by reason through the mediation of sensation. Thus knowledge of conjunction, separation, motion, and rest is attained by reason through the mediation of sensation, since these things do not become tangible, like knowledge of heat, cold, contact, roughness, hardness, and softness. And upon vision, these things—i.e., motion, rest, conjunction, and separation—become visible, according to what we have established. Then, upon stopping at this true statement: It is said to them: If you mean that color is only perceived by sight and is not sensed by any other sense, then the same rule applies to motion, rest, conjunction, and separation; so there is no specificity for color. And if you mean that there is no way of knowing it except vision, while motion, rest, conjunction, and separation have another way of knowing besides vision, we say: The essence of God—may He be exalted—is known by reason through the meditation of signs that indicate Him. So if it is not necessary for atoms—i.e., motion and rest—to be a color because they are visible, since they are known by another way in general in sensation, then it is not necessary for the Exalted and Majestic Creator to be a color even if He is visible, because He is known by another way. For if it were necessary that He be a color because He is visible, even though there is another way of knowing Him besides vision, then it would be necessary for atoms to be colors because they are visible even though there is another way of knowing them. To say this is a departure from knowledge. So it is with this.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponents' ignorance of realities. The opponents claim that color is only perceived by sight, whereas motion, rest, conjunction (gathering), and separation (dispersion) are perceived by touch as well as sight. Nasafi first asks them to clarify what they mean: Do they mean that these accidents are actually touched and become tangible, or that they are known through touch without being tangible? If they mean the former (that they are touched and become tangible), Nasafi refutes this because only bodies can be touched; accidents cannot bear tangence. If they mean the latter (that they become known by sensation through touching the body that is their locus), Nasafi responds that what does not fall directly under sense (like accidents) is not known by sense alone but by reason through the mediation of sense. So knowledge of motion, etc., is rational, not directly sensory. Then he notes that upon vision, these accidents become visible, so they are perceived by sight as well. Nasafi then presents two interpretations: (1) If the opponents mean that color is only perceived by sight and not by other senses, then the same holds for motion, etc. (they are only perceived by sight, not by touch as a separate sense for knowledge), so color has no special exclusivity. (2) If they mean that there is no way to know color except sight, while motion, etc., have another way (touch), then Nasafi applies this to God: God's essence is known by reason through signs, not only by vision. So being visible does not make God a color, just as motion being visible does not make it a color, since both have another way of being known. If being visible necessitated being a color, then motion would also be a color, which is absurd.

عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that motion is directly felt by touch, like a tangible object.
Correction: Nasafi explains that motion is not tangible; it is inferred by reason through touching the moving body.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, when the opponents say that motion is 'perceived by touch', what is the first possible meaning he refutes?
A) Motion becomes a tangible body when touched.
B) Motion is known by reason through touch.
C) Motion is not perceived by sight.
D) Motion is an attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Motion and similar accidents are known by reason through the senses, not directly by touch as tangible objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you touch a moving hand, do you truly feel the motion itself, or does your mind infer motion from the changing pressure? How many of your everyday certainties are actually rational judgments rather than direct sensations? Will you begin to distinguish between what you sense and what you conclude?
?
Nasafi argues that accidents like motion are known by reason through touch. If your senses can deceive you about something so basic, how much more careful should you be about relying solely on your feelings in matters of faith? What assumption about your own perception are you willing to challenge today?
?
You have likely claimed to 'know' something because you experienced it physically. But this lesson shows that even physical perception involves rational inference. Where in your life have you mistaken an inference for a direct experience? Commit to examining one such assumption this week.

Why God's Visibility Does Not Imply Color
Nasafi argues that God's visibility does not entail color, analogizing with motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the analogy of motion to argue that God can be visible without being a color.

Core Idea
Being visible does not make something a color; God is known by reason, not only sight.

Having refuted the claim about motion's perception, Nasafi now applies the same logic to the divine attribute of visibility. He argues that if motion can be visible without being a color because it is known by another way, then God can also be visible without being a color, since He is known by reason. This lesson shows the careful analogical reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional argument
Nasafi presents two interpretations of the opponents' claim about color being only perceived by sight. He analogizes with motion to show that color has no special exclusivity.
Pay attention to the logical structure: If they mean A, then same rule; if they mean B, then absurd conclusion.
2
Apply the analogy to God
Nasafi argues that just as motion is visible but not a color because it has another way of being known, God is visible but not a color because He is known by reason through signs.
This refutes the opponents' implication that visibility implies color.
3
Recognize the final conclusion
To claim that being visible requires being a color would force the absurdity that motion is also a color, which is a departure from knowledge.
Nasafi ends with a strong refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Visibility from Existence, Not Color
Nasafi argues visibility stems from existence, not color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that visibility is a consequence of existence, not of being a color, and how this refutes the idea that God's visibility implies He is a physical body.

Core Idea
Visibility follows existence, not color; otherwise, all non-sensory things would be colors.

Nasafi begins by reaffirming the proof that visibility is a ruling of existence. He then challenges the alternative view that visibility is tied to being a color, showing its absurd consequences. This sets the stage for defending God's visibility without attributing physicality to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof that visibility depends on existence
Read the first few sentences where Nasafi states that visibility is a ruling of existence, both for God and for colors. Notice how he uses the example of color to illustrate the principle.
The key point: the ruling indicates its cause (existence), not a restricted existent.
2
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi shows that if visibility were tied to being non-perceptible by other senses, then abstract entities like knowledge and power would have to be colors, which is false. Trace this argument step by step.
This refutes the notion that visibility implies color or physicality.
3
Summarize the positive proofs for God's visibility
Nasafi quickly recaps that he has proven God is not a color, that vision is possible for Him, and that taste, smell etc. are impossible. This reinforces that God's visibility is from existence alone.
The conclusion: color is not defined by being exclusively visible.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الوجود إذ الحكم يدل على علته لا على موجود مقيد، ولا دلالة في كونه مرئيا إلا على مطلق الوجود، فلو كان لونا لكان لا لأنه مرئي بل لأنه لا يدرك بالحواس الآخر، فيكون كونه [؟] حكما لكونه غير مدرك بالحواس الذوق والشم واللمس والسمع مستحيل على ذاته، وأثبتنا بالدليل أنه ليس بلون، فكان من مقتضى مجموع هذه الدلائل أن اللون ليس ما يختص إدراكه بالرؤية لقيام الدليل على ذات مختص بكونه مرئيا وليس بلون. شبهة لهم: قالوا تعليق جواز الرؤية بالوجود فاسد، فإن كثيرا من الموجودات لا تتعلق بها الرؤية كالقدر والإرادات والعلوم، والاعتقادات والفكر والطعوم والروائح، فإذا كل مرئي وإن كان موجودا فكل موجود ليس بمرئي، فوقع الانفكاك بين الوجود والرؤية. قلنا : التعليل وقع لجواز الرؤية لا للوجود ، وهذه الأشياء جائزة الرؤية بالدليل الذي تقدم ذكره، فأما وجوب الرؤية فإنه يكون بتخليق الله تعالى للرؤية في آلة الرؤية فإذا خلق الرؤية للشيء يرى ذلك، وإن لم يخلق الرؤية وخلق ضدها لا يرى، ولم يخرج الشيء من أن يكون مرئيا هذا كما أن الله تعالى لو خلق العلم بشيء من الأشياء علم ذلك الشيء، ولو لم يخلق العلم حتى جهله الإنسان بقي مجهولا، ولم يخرج من أن يكون العلم به ممكنا، فكذا هذا. فإن قالوا: هذا الكلام في غاية الفساد فإن وجود المرئي وارتفاع السواتر بينه وبين آلة الرؤية بدون الرؤية لا يتصور. قلنا: لم قلتم ذلك؟ فإن قالوا: لو جاز هذا لبطلت المعارف وصحت السوفسطائية فإن على قود كلامكم هذا يجوز أن يكون ها هنا بحضرتنا قبله عظام ترقص ونيران هائلة تضطرم و دبادب وبوقات تضرب وينفخ فيها، وأعلام منشورة وعساكر مارة، ولم تخلق فينا الرؤية والسمع، فلم نقف على ذلك، وركوب هذا وتجويزه خروج عن المعقول وادعاء ما يعرف كذبه ضرورة وتمسك بالسوفسطائية. قلنا: شيء مما ذكرت ليس بلازم فإن الرؤية إنما كانت معنى في الآلة يخلقه الله تعالى عند فتح الإنسان العين لا محالة بلا خلاف بيننا وبين المعتزلة سوى أبي هاشم فإنه أنكر كون الرؤية وسائر الادراكات معانی، ونحن نستدل على ثبوتها عليه بمثل ما نستدل به على ثبوت جميع الأعراض، ولم يحصل لأبي هاشم بهذا
Then we say: We have shown by proof that the being of the essence as visible is a ruling of its being existent, and the Creator exists so He is visible; and color exists so its being visible is a ruling of its being existent, so it is visible because it is existent not because it is a color. So it indicates by its being visible absolute existence, because the ruling indicates its cause, not a restricted existent; and there is no indication in its being visible except for absolute existence. If it were a color, it would not be because it is visible, but because it is not perceived by the other senses, so its being [؟] would be a ruling of its being not perceived by the four other senses besides sight, and this is corrupt, because this is attaching the ruling—its being a color—to non-existence—its being not perceived by those senses—and that is false; and because it would entail that knowledges, power, and wills are all colors because they are not perceived by any of the four senses, so they would be equal to it in the cause of being colors, which is their being not perceived by the four senses, so they would be equal to it in the ruling, and this is false. And because we have shown by proof that its being possible of vision is from the ruling of existence and He exists; and we have proven by proof that taste, smell, touch, and hearing are impossible for His essence; and we have proven by proof that He is not a color; so it follows from the sum of these proofs that color is not that which is exclusively perceived by sight, because the proof has been established for an essence characterized by being visible without being a color. An objection of theirs: They said: Attaching the possibility of vision to existence is corrupt, because many existent things are not subject to vision, such as powers, wills, knowledges, beliefs, thought, tastes, and smells. So even though every visible is existent, not every existent is visible, so there is disjunction between existence and vision. We said: The attribution is for the possibility of vision, not for existence; and these things are possible of vision by the proof mentioned earlier. As for the actual occurrence of vision, it is by God's creation of vision in the organ of vision; so if He creates vision for a thing, it is seen, and if He does not create vision but creates its opposite, it is not seen, and the thing does not cease to be visible. This is like how if God creates knowledge of a thing, one knows that thing, and if He does not create knowledge so that the person remains ignorant of it, it remains unknown, but it does not cease to be knowable. So likewise this. If they say: This speech is extremely corrupt, because the existence of the visible and the removal of obstacles between it and the organ of vision without vision is inconceivable. We say: Why do you say that? If they say: If this were permissible, then knowledge would be invalid and sophistry would be correct, because according to your speech, it would be possible that here in our presence there are huge bones dancing, tremendous fires blazing, drums and trumpets being beaten and blown, unfurled banners and passing armies, while vision and hearing were not created in us, so we would not be aware of that; and adopting and allowing this is a departure from reason, a claim of what is known necessarily to be false, and adherence to sophistry. We say: None of what you mentioned is necessary, because vision is a meaning in the organ that God creates inevitably when a person opens his eyes, without disagreement between us and the Mu'tazila except for Abu Hashim, for he denied that vision and all perceptions are meanings; and we argue for their existence against him by the same sort of proof we use for the existence of all accidents; and Abu Hashim did not achieve anything by this.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument and refutation. First, Nasafi restates his proof that visibility follows from existence, not from being a color. He argues that if something were a color because it is not perceived by other senses, then many non-sensory things like knowledge and will would have to be colors, which is absurd. After summarizing his proofs that God is not a color and that vision is possible, he presents the objection: many existent things (powers, wills, etc.) are not visible, so existence does not entail visibility. Nasafi replies that the attribution is to possibility, not actuality; those things are possible to see in principle, but actual vision depends on God creating sight. He uses an analogy: just as something remains knowable even if not known, so it remains visible even if not seen. The objectors then raise a sophistical scenario about sensing huge events without perception, which Nasafi dismisses by noting that vision is a created meaning that God inevitably creates when eyes open, according to standard Sunni and Mu'tazili view (except Abu Hashim). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
وجود (wujud)
لون (lawn)
تعليق (ta'liq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is visible, He must be a physical body with color.
Correction: Nasafi argues visibility follows from existence, not from being a color or having physical properties.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if visibility were caused by being non-perceptible by the four other senses?
A) God would be a color.
B) Knowledge, power, and wills would have to be colors.
C) Colors would be invisible.
D) Visibility would be impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
Visibility is a consequence of existence, not of being a color or any physical attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah in the afterlife, does your mind involuntarily assign a color or form to that vision? How does Nasafi's argument challenge this instinct, and what does that reveal about your reliance on physical analogies for spiritual realities?
?
If visibility is a direct consequence of existence, then everything that exists is in principle visible to the appropriate observer. How does this reshape your understanding of the 'unseen' (ghayb) and your conviction that Allah sees you at every moment?
?
Nasafi warns against attaching a ruling (visibility) to non-existence (not being perceived by other senses). In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes define your faith by what you avoid or lack (e.g., 'I do not commit major sins') rather than by the positive existence of iman and tawhid? What would it mean to shift your focus to existence-based attributes?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Refuting the Objection: Many Existents Are Invisible
Nasafi refutes the objection from non-visible existents and the sophistical scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that many existent things are not visible does not disprove the possibility of seeing God, and how Nasafi distinguishes between possibility and actuality.

Core Idea
Possibility of vision is universal; actual vision depends on God's creation of sight.

The objectors argue that since many existent things (like knowledge, power) are not visible, existence does not entail visibility. Nasafi responds by clarifying that the attribution is to possibility, not actual occurrence. He then addresses the further objection that this would allow absurd scenarios, showing that vision is a created meaning that God inevitably provides when conditions are met.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Nasafi's initial reply
The objectors list many existents that are not seen. Nasafi corrects that the attribution is to the possibility of vision, not to existence itself. He asserts those things are possible to see in principle.
The proof mentioned earlier likely refers to the argument that any existent can be seen in principle.
2
Understand the analogy with knowledge
Nasafi compares vision to knowledge: just as something remains knowable even if not known, so it remains visible even if not seen. Actual vision requires God's creation of sight.
This shows that possibility is not negated by absence of actual perception.
3
Examine the sophistical objection and Nasafi's dismissal
The objectors claim that if actual vision is not guaranteed, then anything could be present without our knowledge. Nasafi replies that vision is inevitably created when eyes open, so such scenarios do not occur. He also notes the exception of Abu Hashim who denied that perceptions are meanings.
Nasafi grounds his reply in the standard kalam view of perception as an accident (meaning) created by God.

ثم نقول: إنا بينا بالدليل أن كون الذات مرئيا حكم كونه موجودا، والباري موجود فكان مرئيا، واللون موجود فكان كونه مرئيا حكما لكونه موجودا، فكان مرئيا لكونه موجودا لا لكونه لونا، فدل بكونه مرئيا على مطلق الوجود إذ الحكم يدل على علته لا على موجود مقيد، ولا دلالة في كونه مرئيا إلا على مطلق الوجود، فلو كان لونا لكان لا لأنه مرئي بل لأنه لا يدرك بالحواس الآخر، فيكون كونه [؟] حكما لكونه غير مدرك بالحواس الأربع الأخر سوى البصر، وهذا فاسد، لأن هذا تعليق الحكم وهو كونه لونا بالعدم وهو كونه غير مدرك بتلك الحواس وذا باطل، ولأنه يقتضي أن يكون العلوم والقدر والإرادات كلها ألوانا لأنها لا تدرك بشيء من الحواس الانفكاك بين الوجود والرؤية. قلنا : التعليل وقع لجواز الرؤية لا للوجود ، وهذه الأشياء جائزة الرؤية بالدليل الذي تقدم ذكره، فأما وجوب الرؤية فإنه يكون بتخليق الله تعالى للرؤية في آلة الرؤية فإذا خلق الرؤية للشيء يرى ذلك، وإن لم يخلق الرؤية وخلق ضدها لا يرى، ولم يخرج الشيء من أن يكون مرئيا هذا كما أن الله تعالى لو خلق العلم بشيء من الأشياء علم ذلك الشيء، ولو لم يخلق العلم حتى جهله الإنسان بقي الرؤية والسمع، فلم نقف على ذلك، وركوب هذا وتجويزه خروج عن المعقول وادعاء ما يعرف كذبه ضرورة وتمسك بالسوفسطائية. قلنا: شيء مما ذكرت ليس بلازم فإن الرؤية إنما كانت معنى في الآلة يخلقه الله تعالى عند فتح الإنسان العين لا محالة بلا خلاف بيننا وبين المعتزلة سوى أبي هاشم فإنه أنكر كون الرؤية وسائر الادراكات معانی، ونحن نستدل على ثبوتها عليه بمثل ما نستدل به على ثبوت جميع الأعراض، ولم يحصل لأبي هاشم بهذا
Then we say: We have shown by proof that the being of the essence as visible is a ruling of its being existent, and the Creator exists so He is visible; and color exists so its being visible is a ruling of its being existent, so it is visible because it is existent not because it is a color. So it indicates by its being visible absolute existence, because the ruling indicates its cause, not a restricted existent; and there is no indication in its being visible except for absolute existence. Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the existence of many non-visible things not disprove the possibility of seeing God?
A) Because those things are actually visible but we lack the sense.
B) Because the objection confuses the possibility of vision with actual vision.
C) Because those things are not truly existent.
D) Because only colors can be visible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God can be seen in the afterlife, but does your daily life reflect a longing for that vision? ?
Nasafi argues that the possibility of vision is universal, but actual vision depends on God's creation. How often do you take for granted the sights you see every day? If God withheld your sight for a single moment, what would you realize about your absolute dependence on Him?
?
The objectors accused Nasafi of leading to sophistry. When you encounter arguments that challenge your deepest beliefs, do you dismiss them as sophistry without engagement, or do you respond like Nasafi with reasoned distinction? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in God's clarity and your own intellectual humility?
