The Mu'tazila Objection on Pointing and Its Refutation
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila objection about pointing and gives two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection regarding pointing to the visible and the Maturidi response that distinguishes between visibility and spatial extension.

Core Idea
Visibility does not imply pointability; pointing requires spatial extension.

The Mu'tazila argued that if Allah were visible, He could be pointed to, but since He cannot be pointed to, He cannot be seen. In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents two responses: the Ash'ari position allowing directionless pointing, and the Maturidi position that pointing and visibility are separate attributes. He also offers a counter-argument exposing the flaw in the Mu'tazila's analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to grasp the flow of the argument before analysis.
Notice the compact style of argumentation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
Find the objection: 'every visible thing can be pointed to, so if Allah were visible, He could be pointed to.' Understand the implied conclusion that vision is impossible.
3
Understand the first response (Ash'ari)
The Ash'ari response allows pointing without direction, analogous to vision without direction. This blocks the objection for them.
4
Understand the second response (Maturidi)
The Maturidi position rejects pointing to Allah entirely. The key point: pointing in the world is due to spatial extension, not to visibility.
5
Examine the counter-argument
Nasafi turns the tables: if the Mu'tazila's rule applies, then Allah as commander would also need to be pointed to. Their own logic would force them to accept pointing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها أنهم يقولون : كل مرئي يجوز أن يشار إليه والله تعالى لو كان مرئيا لكان يجوز أن يشار إليه. والجواب عنه : أن بعض أصحاب الرؤية وهم الأشعرية يجوزون الإشارة إلى الله تعالى لا إلى جهة كما تجوز الرؤية لا في جهة فكان الإلزام عنهم مندفعا. ثم عندنا وإن كان لا يشار إليه، وإليه ذهب أبو العباس القلانسي، ولكن في الشاهد ما كان جواز الإشارة إلى شيء لجواز الرؤية بل لكونه متحيزا في جهة، فكان جواز الإشارة مع وجود الرؤية من أوصاف الوجود فبطل الإلزام. ثم يعارضون فيقال لهم : كل مخاطب آمر ناه في الشاهد يشار إليه، فلو كان الله تعالى مخاطبا آمرا ناهيا لكان يشار إليه. فما أجابوا به عنه فهو لهم جواب.
Among them is that they say: Every visible thing is permissible to be pointed to, and if God Most High were visible, He would be permissible to be pointed to. The answer to it: Some of the proponents of vision, namely the Ash‘arites, permit pointing to God Most High without direction, just as vision is permitted without direction, so the implication from them is repelled. Then according to us, even though He is not pointed to—and Abu al-‘Abbas al-Qalanisi held this view—nevertheless, in the witnessed world, the permissibility of pointing to a thing is not due to the permissibility of vision, but rather due to its being extended in a direction. Thus, permissibility of pointing together with the existence of vision is among the attributes of existence; so the implication is invalid. Then they are countered by saying to them: Every addressed commander or prohibitor in the witnessed world is pointed to, so if God Most High were an addressed commander and prohibitor, He would be pointed to. Whatever they answer with about that is for them an answer.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from the Mu‘tazila against the vision of Allah (ru’ya) and the response. The objection is: everything visible can be pointed to; if Allah were visible, He could be pointed to. But they deny pointing to Allah, so vision is impossible. The response has two parts. First, the Ash‘arites (who affirm vision) allow pointing to Allah without a direction, just as vision is without a direction, so the objection does not apply to them. Second, the Maturidi position (of the author and Abu al-‘Abbas al-Qalanisi) holds that Allah is not pointed to at all. They argue that in the witnessed world, the permissibility of pointing is not due to visibility but due to being extended in space. Visibility and pointing are both attributes of existence, but they do not entail each other. So the implication fails. Then a counter-argument is given: if the Mu‘tazila claim that every addressed commander/prohibitor is pointed to in the witnessed world, then by analogy Allah, who addresses and commands, would have to be pointed to. This exposes inconsistency in their position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on pointing to Allah are identical.
Correction: The Ash'ari allow pointing without direction, while the Maturidi (with Qalanisi) reject pointing entirely. The passage shows this difference.

Checkpoint

A) They agree that pointing implies visibility but deny that Allah is visible.
B) They argue that permissibility of pointing in the witnessed world is due to being extended in space, not due to visibility.
C) They accept the objection and conclude that Allah cannot be seen.
D) They say that pointing is possible for Allah without direction.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection fails because pointing and visibility are not necessarily linked; pointing requires spatial extension, which does not apply to Allah.

Visibility Does Not Imply Location
Nasafi refutes the claim that every visible thing must be in a place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that every visible thing must be in a place is false, and how Nasafi refutes it with counterexamples and a counter-argument.

Core Idea
Visibility and location are not necessarily linked.

The Mu'tazila argued that if Allah were visible, He would have to be in a place, which is impossible. Nasafi responds by showing that visibility does not depend on location. He gives clear counterexamples from both the physical world and theological analogy. This lesson walks through that response step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the Mu'tazila claim: every visible thing is in a place. This is the premise they use to deny the vision of Allah.
The objection is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Examine the direct refutation
Nasafi gives three counterexamples to show that visibility does not require location. Identify each one: an indivisible atom, the upper surface of the world, and invisible things that occupy place.
These examples directly disprove the premise.
3
Understand the counter-argument (mu'āraḍa)
Nasafi then uses an analogy: in the witnessed world, a commander/prohibitor is seen only in a place, but Allah is a commander/prohibitor yet not in a place. Similarly, living beings and agents in the witnessed world are bodies in place, but Allah is not a body, so the analogy does not force the conclusion.
This shows that the attribute of being seen does not necessarily entail location.
4
Connect to the key takeaway
Reflect on the conclusion: the original objection fails because visibility and location are not necessarily linked. This preserves the possibility of seeing Allah without spatial constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك قولهم : كل مرئي في مكان. فيقال لهم ما كان في المكان لأنه مرئي، وما كان مرئيا لأنه في مكان، فإنه تعالى لو خلق جزء لا يتجزأ لا في مكان لكان مرئيا والصفحة العليا من العالم مرئية وهي لا في مكان، وقد يحل في المكان ما ليس بمرئي عندكم، كالعلوم والقدر والإرادات وغيرها فبطل قولهم. ثم يعارضون بالمخاطب الآمر الناهي أنه لم ير في الشاهد إلا في مكان وفي الغائب آمر ناه مخاطب ليس في مكان، وكذا لا حي ولا فاعل في الشاهد إلا في مكان وهو محدود متناه جسم، ولا جسم، ولا يجب التعدية إلى الغائب فكذا فيما نحن فيه.
And among that is their statement: Every visible thing is in a place. So it is said to them: It was not in a place because it is visible, nor was it visible because it is in a place. For He Most High, if He created an indivisible part not in a place, it would be visible; and the upper surface of the world is visible while it is not in a place; and sometimes what is not visible according to you occupies a place, such as knowledges, power, wills, and others. So their statement is invalid. Then they are countered by the addressed commander/prohibitor: he is not seen in the witnessed world except in a place, while in the unseen there is a commander, prohibitor, addressee not in a place. Similarly, no living being and no agent in the witnessed world is except in a place, and it is limited, finite, a body, and not a body; and transference to the unseen is not necessary. Thus it is in what we are discussing.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage addresses the second Mu‘tazila objection: every visible thing is in a place, so if Allah were visible He would be in a place, which is impossible. The response is twofold. First, a direct refutation: visibility does not depend on being in a place. Counterexamples include an indivisible atom (if created) not in a place but visible, and the upper surface of the world (the celestial sphere) which is visible but not located in a place (in the sense of being spatially contained). Also, according to the Mu‘tazila, attributes like knowledge and power occupy a place (in the heart or brain) but are not visible. Thus the original claim is false. Second, a counter-argument (mu‘āraḍa): the Mu‘tazila accept that Allah is an addressed commander/prohibitor, but in the witnessed world such a being is only seen in a place. Yet they affirm that Allah is not in a place, so by analogy the same could hold for vision: just as the attribute of being an addressed commander does not require location in the unseen, so vision does not require location. They also argue that in the witnessed world all living beings and agents are bodies in a place, but Allah is not a body, so the analogy does not force the conclusion that He must be seen in a place.

Key Terms
مكان
مرئي
جزء لا يتجزأ
الصفحة العليا
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visibility necessarily implies being in a place.
Correction: Nasafi provides counterexamples such as an indivisible atom not in place yet visible, and the upper surface of the world visible but not spatially contained, showing that visibility can occur without location.

Checkpoint
What example does Nasafi give to show that something can be visible without being in a place?
A) A body in a vacuum
B) An indivisible atom created not in a place
C) The color black
D) A thought in the mind

Key Takeaway
Visibility does not require location; the Mu'tazila objection fails because counterexamples show the two are not necessarily linked.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously imagine Allah as limited by space because everything you see seems to occupy a place? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for how you think of Allah's presence?
?
When you hear that Allah will be seen in the afterlife, do you struggle to reconcile that with His transcendence? This passage argues that visibility does not require location—does that change your anticipation of that vision? If so, how?
?
Are there aspects of your faith where you have compared Allah to created things without realizing it? This refutation shows the danger of such analogies. Will you commit to studying more deeply the attributes of Allah to avoid falling into anthropomorphism?

Nasafi's Reductio on Seeing Allah
Nasafi's reductio against Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a reductio argument to demonstrate that the Mu'tazila's position on the impossibility of seeing Allah leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Limited experience cannot limit what is possible for Allah.

The Mu'tazila argued that since Allah is not seen in this world, He cannot be seen in the Hereafter. Nasafi counters this by showing that if we follow that logic consistently, it would also lead to denying many other visible things. In this passage, he uses a vivid hypothetical example to expose the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the hypothetical scenario about a black person and black bodies.
2
Identify the reductio example
The passage presents a hypothetical situation where a person's experience is limited to only black things. Understand what the person would conclude based on that limited experience.
The conclusion is that nothing else exists except black things.
3
Trace the absurd consequence
See how the person's limited experience leads them to deny the existence of other colors, bodies, etc. This is clearly false by rational consensus.
The absurdity is that the principle of limited experience would deny ordinary visible things.
4
Apply to the Mu'tazila's position
Understand that the Mu'tazila use a similar argument: because we don't see Allah now, He cannot be seen at all. The reductio shows this reasoning is flawed because it would lead to absurdities if applied consistently.
The same logic that denies Allah's visibility would also deny the visibility of many other things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا قياد كلامهم يلزم أن الله تعالى لو لم يخلق إلا زنجيا، أو لم ير إنسان إلا زنجيا أو لم ير إلا جسما أسود، ولم يخلق الله تعالى من الألوان إلا السواد. يعتقد أن لا موجود إلا زنجي ولا مرئي إلا زنجي، ولا جسم إلا أسود، ولا لون إلا سواد، ولا يجوز وجود ما وراء هذه الأشياء ولا رؤية غيرها لتعلق الوجود والرؤية بها. وهذا كله باطل بإجماع العقلاء فكذا ما تمسكت به المعتزلة من أنواع هذه الخيالات.
And similarly, the logical consequence of their speech would entail that if God Most High had created only a black person, or if a person had never seen except a black person, or had never seen except a black body, and God had created only blackness among colors, he would believe that nothing exists except a black person, nothing is visible except a black person, no body except black, no color except black, and he would not allow the existence of anything beyond these things nor the seeing of anything else, because existence and vision are attached to them. All this is false by consensus of rational people. Similarly, what the Mu‘tazila have clung to from these types of fantasies.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu‘tazila's reasoning about the conditions of visibility. The author argues that if we follow the Mu‘tazila's method—namely, that whatever is not seen in our experience cannot be seen at all—then if God had created only one type of thing (e.g., only a black person, only black bodies, only the color black), and a person had only ever perceived that type, that person would conclude that nothing else exists or is visible. This would lead to denying the existence of white bodies, other colors, etc., which is clearly false by rational consensus. Thus, the Mu‘tazila's argument that Allah cannot be seen because we do not see Him in our ordinary experience is equally invalid. The argument shows that their reasoning leads to absurd consequences when generalized.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila argument only concerns God, not ordinary objects.
Correction: The reductio shows that their principle would also deny the visibility of ordinary objects if applied consistently, revealing its flaw.

Key Takeaway

?

Refuting the Objection of Dissimilarity to Vision of Allah
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili objection that dissimilarity prevents vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that Allah's dissimilarity to visible objects prevents His being seen.

Core Idea
Dissimilarity does not prevent vision; opposition (ḍidd) is a stronger factor, yet opposites are seen.

The Mu'tazila argued that since only certain genera (bodies, colors) are visible, and Allah is utterly dissimilar to them, He cannot be seen. Nasafi responds by showing that dissimilarity is not the cause of invisibility; rather, the presence of an opposite (such as between black and white) can be present without preventing vision. This lesson unpacks his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili objection
Understand their reasoning: vision attaches to specific genera; tastes/smells are not seen due to dissimilarity; Allah is even more dissimilar.
Note the claim that dissimilarity is the cause of non-vision.
2
Examine Nasafi's alternative explanation
He argues that the real reason tastes/smells are not seen is the existence of an opposite (ḍidd), not dissimilarity. Black and white are opposites yet both seen.
Opposition is stronger than dissimilarity.
3
Draw the conclusion for Allah's vision
Since dissimilarity is not the cause, the argument that Allah cannot be seen due to dissimilarity collapses.
The Maturidi position that Allah is visible remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا القبيل قولهم : إن الرؤية لما تعلقت بهذه الأجناس المخصوصة، ولم ير ما سواها من الطعوم والروائح إنما لم ير لمخالفتها هذه الأجناس المخصوصة، والمخالفة بين الله تعالى وبين هذه الأجناس فوق المخالفة بين الطعوم والروائح وبين هذه الأجناس (لأنه لا شبهة بين الله تعالى وبين خلقه يوجه من الوجوه وبين الطعوم والروائح، وبين هذه الأجناس مشابهة من وجوه كالحديث والعرضية وأشباه ذلك). والجواب: أن انعدام رؤية هذه الأشياء ما كان لما ذكرتم بل لوجود ضد فعندنا مرئية، والدليل عليه أنا نرى السواد ونرى البياض أيضا مع رؤيتنا، فأما هي * وجود التضاد بينهما وهو أقوى من المخالفة، فدل أن انعدام رؤية ما انعدمت رؤيته ما كان لمكان المخالفة بل لما بينا وأعرضت عن ذكر مثل هذه الأسئلة لاتضاح طريق الدفع والتحامي عن الإطالة.
And of this kind is their statement: Since vision is attached to these specific genera (colors and bodies), and what other than them—such as tastes and smells—is not seen, it is only not seen because of their dissimilarity to these specific genera. And the dissimilarity between God Most High and these genera is greater than the dissimilarity between tastes and smells and these genera (because there is no similarity at all between God Most High and His creation in any respect, while between tastes and smells and these genera there is similarity in some respects, such as being originated and being accidental, and the like). The answer: The absence of vision of these things was not for what you mentioned, but rather due to the existence of an opposite, for they are in principle visible. The evidence for that is that we see black and we also see white along with our seeing; their existence involves opposition (tadād) between them, which is stronger than dissimilarity. This indicates that the absence of vision of what is not seen was not due to dissimilarity, but due to what we stated. And I refrain from mentioning such questions because the method of refutation is clear and to avoid prolongation.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage addresses a third Mu‘tazila objection: visible things in our experience belong to specific genera (bodies, colors). Tastes and smells are not visible because they are dissimilar to these genera. Since Allah is even more dissimilar to bodies and colors than tastes and smells are, He cannot be seen. The response is that the reason tastes and smells are not seen is not dissimilarity but the existence of an opposite (ḍidd). The author argues that black and white are both seen despite being opposite to each other; the opposition (tadād) prevents simultaneous perception in the same sense? Actually he says 'the existence of opposition between them is stronger than dissimilarity' meaning that despite dissimilarity, they are both visible. The real reason for non-vision of tastes and smells is that they are not colored bodies or have opposition? Wait, the reasoning is: white and black are both seen even though they are opposites (ḍidd). Since opposition is a stronger obstacle than dissimilarity, and yet they are seen, dissimilarity cannot be the cause of non-vision. So the Mu‘tazila's argument fails. The author then says he will not dwell on such questions as the method of refutation is clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dissimilarity is the cause of invisibility; therefore Allah cannot be seen because He is extremely dissimilar.
Correction: Nasafi argues that dissimilarity is not the cause; instead, the presence of an opposite (ḍidd) can prevent vision, but opposites like black and white are both seen, so dissimilarity is irrelevant.

Refuting the Argument from Limited Perception
Nasafi presents a reductio ad absurdum against Mu'tazili reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazili argument from limited perception leads to absurdity and is refuted by consensus of rational people.

Core Idea
If perception were limited by what exists, absurd consequences follow — so the argument fails.

The Mu'tazila argue that since we only perceive what exists, and God is unlike anything we know, He cannot be seen. The author demonstrates that this reasoning, if taken to its logical extreme, leads to patently false conclusions. He uses a reductio ad absurdum to expose its weakness.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio example
Read the hypothetical scenario where only a black person/objects exist. Notice how the Mu'tazili logic would force an absurd belief.
The example is deliberately extreme to highlight the flaw.
2
Observe the conclusion
The author declares the result 'bāṭil bi-ijmāʿ al-ʿuqalā' (false by consensus of rational people). This is the refutation.
Consensus of rational people is a strong argument in kalām.
3
Understand the refutation's force
The Mu'tazili argument is reduced to an absurdity; thus, it cannot be used to deny ru'ya. The reductio shows the premise is faulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكان وهو محدود متناه جسم، ولا جسم، ولا يجب التعدية إلى الغائب فكذا فيما نحن فيه. وكذا قياد كلامهم يلزم أن الله تعالى لو لم يخلق إلا زنجيا، أو لم ير إنسان إلا زنجيا أو لم ير إلا جسما أسود، ولم يخلق الله تعالى من الألوان إلا السواد. يعتقد أن لا موجود إلا زنجي ولا مرئي إلا زنجي، ولا جسم إلا أسود، ولا لون إلا سواد، ولا يجوز وجود ما وراء هذه الأشياء ولا رؤية غيرها لتعلق الوجود والرؤية بها. وهذا كله باطل بإجماع العقلاء فكذا ما تمسكت به المعتزلة من أنواع هذه الخيالات. ومن هذا القبيل قولهم : إن الرؤية لما تعلقت بهذه الأجناس المخصوصة، ولم ير ما سواها من الطعوم والروائح إنما لم ير لمخالفتها هذه الأجناس المخصوصة، والمخالفة بين الله تعالى وبين هذه الأجناس فوق المخالفة بين الطعوم والروائح وبين هذه الأجناس لأنه لا شبهة بين الله تعالى وبين خلقه يوجه من الوجوه وبين الطعوم والروائح، وبين هذه الأجناس مشابهة من وجوه كالحديث والعرضية وأشباه ذلك. والجواب: أن انعدام رؤية هذه الأشياء ما كان لما ذكرتم بل لوجود ضد فعندنا مرئية، والدليل عليه أنا نرى السواد ونرى البياض أيضا مع رؤيتنا، فأما هي وجود التضاد بينهما وهو أقوى من المخالفة، فدل أن انعدام رؤية ما انعدمت رؤيته ما كان لمكان المخالفة بل لما بينا وأعرضت عن ذكر مثل هذه الأسئلة لاتضاح طريق الدفع والتحامي عن الإطالة.
...place while it is limited finite body, and not body, and it is not necessary to extend to the unseen, so likewise in what we are discussing. Similarly, the conclusion of their speech necessitates that if God had not created except a black person, or a human had not seen except a black person, or had not seen except a black body, and God had not created from colors except black, he would believe that there exists nothing except a black person, and nothing visible except a black person, and no body except black, and no color except black, and he would not allow the existence of anything beyond these things nor the seeing of anything else because existence and vision are attached to them. And all this is false by consensus of the rational people, so likewise what the Mu'tazila have clung to from these types of fantasies. And of this kind is their saying: 'Since vision attaches to these specific genera, and what is other than them from tastes and smells is not seen, it is only not seen because they differ from these specific genera, and the difference between God and these genera is greater than the difference between tastes and smells and these genera, because there is no similarity between God and His creation in any respect, while between tastes and smells and these genera there is similarity in respects such as contingency and accidentality and the like.' The answer: that the absence of vision of these things was not due to what you mentioned, but rather due to the existence of an opposite, so with us they are visible, and the evidence for that is that we see black and we see white also together with our vision; as for it, it is the existence of opposition between them, which is stronger than difference. So it indicates that the absence of vision of what has its vision absent was not because of difference, but because of what we have explained. And I refrain from mentioning such questions because the way of rebuttal is clear and to avoid prolongation.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage refutes two Mu'tazili arguments used to deny the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya). The first argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if God had created only one type of being (e.g., only black people and black objects), one would conclude that only black exists and is visible. This, the author says, is false by universal consensus, and the Mu'tazila's reliance on such fantasies is likewise invalid. The second argument directly concerns ru'ya: the Mu'tazila claim that vision attaches only to specific genera (e.g., bodies and colors) and not to tastes or smells because the latter differ from those genera; they then argue that God's difference from these genera is even greater, so He cannot be seen. The author answers that the reason tastes and smells are not seen is not due to difference, but because they have opposites (e.g., black and white are opposites, while tastes do not have such visual opposites). The evidence is that we see black and white together, and their opposition is stronger than mere difference. Hence, the absence of vision of something is due to opposition, not difference. The author concludes by stating that he will not address further such questions as the method of refutation is clear and to avoid prolixity.

موجود

إجماع العقلاء

Misconception: The Mu'tazili argument that we cannot see God because our perception is limited to existing things is valid.
Correction: The author shows that this reasoning leads to absurdities when generalized, so it is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili argument from limited perception collapses when its implications are carried to absurdity.

Refuting the Argument from Difference of Genera
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili argument based on difference of genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili argument from difference of genera fails, and why opposition (tadād) rather than difference explains why some things are not seen.

Core Idea
Opposition, not mere difference, is what prevents vision — and God has no opposite, so He can be seen.

The Mu'tazila claim that vision attaches only to specific genera like bodies and colors, and that God's difference from these genera is even greater than that of tastes and smells, so God cannot be seen. The author counters by showing that tastes and smells are not seen because they have opposites, not because they differ. Since God has no opposite, the argument from difference does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim
The Mu'tazila argue that vision attaches to specific genera (bodies, colors); things that differ (like tastes) are not seen; God differs even more, so He cannot be seen.
Notice the analogy: tastes vs. genera, then God vs. genera.
2
Identify the author's answer
The author says the absence of vision is due to opposition (tadād), not difference. Tastes have opposites (e.g., sweet vs. sour?) but the example given is black and white.
Opposition is a stronger factor than difference.
3
Examine the evidence
The author points out that we see black and white together — they are opposites. This shows opposition governs visibility, not difference.
4
Understand the application to God
Since God has no opposite, the reason for non-visibility (opposition) does not apply. Therefore, the Mu'tazili argument fails.
The author ends by saying he will not address further such questions.

مكان وهو محدود متناه جسم، ولا جسم، ولا يجب التعدية إلى الغائب فكذا فيما نحن فيه. وكذا قياد كلامهم يلزم أن الله تعالى لو لم يخلق إلا زنجيا، أو لم ير إنسان إلا زنجيا أو لم ير إلا جسما أسود، ولم يخلق الله تعالى من الألوان إلا السواد. يعتقد أن لا موجود إلا زنجي ولا مرئي إلا زنجي، ولا جسم إلا أسود، ولا لون إلا سواد، ولا يجوز وجود ما وراء هذه الأشياء ولا رؤية غيرها لتعلق الوجود والرؤية بها. وهذا كله باطل بإجماع العقلاء فكذا ما تمسكت به المعتزلة من أنواع هذه الخيالات. ومن هذا القبيل قولهم : إن الرؤية لما تعلقت بهذه الأجناس المخصوصة، ولم ير ما سواها من الطعوم والروائح إنما لم ير لمخالفتها هذه الأجناس المخصوصة، والمخالفة بين الله تعالى وبين هذه الأجناس فوق المخالفة بين الطعوم والروائح وبين هذه الأجناس لأنه لا شبهة بين الله تعالى وبين خلقه يوجه من الوجوه وبين الطعوم والروائح، وبين هذه الأجناس مشابهة من وجوه كالحديث والعرضية وأشباه ذلك. والجواب: أن انعدام رؤية هذه الأشياء ما كان لما ذكرتم بل لوجود ضد فعندنا مرئية، والدليل عليه أنا نرى السواد ونرى البياض أيضا مع رؤيتنا، فأما هي وجود التضاد بينهما وهو أقوى من المخالفة، فدل أن انعدام رؤية ما انعدمت رؤيته ما كان لمكان المخالفة بل لما بينا وأعرضت عن ذكر مثل هذه الأسئلة لاتضاح طريق الدفع والتحامي عن الإطالة.
...place while it is limited finite body, and not body, and it is not necessary to extend to the unseen, so likewise in what we are discussing. Similarly, the conclusion of their speech necessitates that if God had not created except a black person, or a human had not seen except a black person, or had not seen except a black body, and God had not created from colors except black, he would believe that there exists nothing except a black person, and nothing visible except a black person, and no body except black, and no color except black, and he would not allow the existence of anything beyond these things nor the seeing of anything else because existence and vision are attached to them. And all this is false by consensus of the rational people, so likewise what the Mu'tazila have clung to from these types of fantasies. And of this kind is their saying: 'Since vision attaches to these specific genera, and what is other than them from tastes and smells is not seen, it is only not seen because they differ from these specific genera, and the difference between God and these genera is greater than the difference between tastes and smells and these genera, because there is no similarity between God and His creation in any respect, while between tastes and smells and these genera there is similarity in respects such as contingency and accidentality and the like.' The answer: that the absence of vision of these things was not due to what you mentioned, but rather due to the existence of an opposite, so with us they are visible, and the evidence for that is that we see black and we see white also together with our vision; as for it, it is the existence of opposition between them, which is stronger than difference. So it indicates that the absence of vision of what has its vision absent was not because of difference, but because of what we have explained. And I refrain from mentioning such questions because the way of rebuttal is clear and to avoid prolongation.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage refutes two Mu'tazili arguments used to deny the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya). The first argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if God had created only one type of being (e.g., only black people and black objects), one would conclude that only black exists and is visible. This, the author says, is false by universal consensus, and the Mu'tazila's reliance on such fantasies is likewise invalid. The second argument directly concerns ru'ya: the Mu'tazila claim that vision attaches only to specific genera (e.g., bodies and colors) and not to tastes or smells because the latter differ from those genera; they then argue that God's difference from these genera is even greater, so He cannot be seen. The author answers that the reason tastes and smells are not seen is not due to difference, but because they have opposites (e.g., black and white are opposites, while tastes do not have such visual opposites). The evidence is that we see black and white together, and their opposition is stronger than mere difference. Hence, the absence of vision of something is due to opposition, not difference. The author concludes by stating that he will not address further such questions as the method of refutation is clear and to avoid prolixity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili argument from difference of genera is sound because God is unlike anything we see.
Correction: The author shows that the absence of vision of tastes and smells is due to opposition, not difference, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
What does the author give as evidence that opposition is the reason for not seeing something?
A) We see black and white together even though they are opposites
B) Tastes and smells do not exist
C) God has no opposite
D) The Mu'tazila are irrational

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili argument from difference collapses because the real cause of invisibility is opposition, and God has no opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila mistakenly thought that difference prevents vision. Do you ever treat people who are different from you as if they are opponents, when in reality their differences don't block your ability to see their humanity? Name one relationship where you can replace opposition with acceptance this week.
?
Since God has no opposite, nothing can prevent you from 'seeing' Him except your own heedlessness. What imaginary opposites (like distractions or false priorities) have you placed between yourself and the awareness of His presence? Write down one concrete step to remove that barrier today.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
Refuting Mu'tazili Conditions for Vision
Refutes Mu'tazili conditions for vision using the visibility of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili conditions for vision are invalid, especially when applied to accidents, and why they cannot be prerequisites for seeing God.

Core Idea
Conditions for vision fail because accidents are seen without them.

The Mu'tazila claimed that vision requires specific physical conditions like distance, a ray, imprinting, removal of veils, facing, and the seen being a body. In this passage, the author argues that these conditions cannot be necessary because accidents—which are not bodies—are still seen. This forces the Mu'tazila to either deny the visibility of accidents (which they concede) or abandon their conditions. The passage systematically dismantles each condition by showing it is inapplicable to accidents, thereby clearing the way for the possibility of seeing God without such constraints.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author lists the Mu'tazili conditions and then refutes them, using the visibility of accidents as the key rebuttal.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the conditions refuted
List all the conditions the author mentions: distance, ray, imprinting, veils, being located in space, and facing. See how the author attacks each one.
3
Understand the accident argument
The core argument is that accidents are seen without these conditions. The Mu'tazila themselves accept that accidents are visible, so the conditions cannot be universal. Focus on how each condition fails for accidents.
Note the example of the mirror: imprinting is not actual but a creation of likeness.
4
Examine the refutation of facing
The author singles out facing (muqabala) and shows it is only between bodies, not accidents. Since accidents are seen, facing cannot be a necessary condition for vision in general.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that everything is seen as it is, without change. This reinforces that vision does not impose conditions on the seen. Ask yourself: How does this support the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that God can be seen without these conditions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعلهم المسافة بين الرائي والمرئي واتصال الشعاع وانطباع المرئي في عين الرائي وارتفاع الحجب شرطا للرؤية فاسد، وكذا اشتراطهم كون المرئي متحيزا فاسد، لأنهم سلموا لنا رؤية الأعراض، ومن لم يسلم منهم ذلك ألزمناه بالدليل، واتصال الشعاع بالأعراض غير متصور ، وكذا إدراكها بالمماسة على ما قررنا. ومن مذهب النظام أن الشعاع جسم يماس المرئي والعرض لا يماس ومع دل أن هذا باطل وكذا انطباع المرئي، لأن المرئي لا ينطبع ولكن يخلق الله تعالى مثل صورة المرئي في المرآة، ولا صورة للأعراض. وكذا ارتفاع الحجب تكون فيما بين الأجسام، فأما في حق الأعراض فلا يقال: ارتفعت الحجب إلا أن ترتفع عن محلها. وقد بينا قبل هذا أن ارتفاع الحجب عن محالها لا أثر له في حقها، على أنا بينا أن الحجاب لا أثر له في المنع عن الرؤية فلم يكن لارتفاعه أثر في إثبات الرؤية. وكذا جعلهم المقابلة شرطا فاسد لأن المقابلة تكون ثابتة بين الأجسام، فأما الأعراض فلا تقابل ولا تقابل وقد ثبت رؤيتها، دل أنها ليست بشرط غير أنها وقعت في حق الأجسام لضرورة كونها متحيزة متناهية، وكل شيء يرى كما هو لا توجب الرؤية تبدلا في المرئي، فإن الأسود يرى أسود والأبيض يرى أبيض، وكذا المتحرك والساكن والطويل والقصير والعريض والمثلث والمربع والمدور والجماد والحيوان، وكل ما يعاين ويرى من الأجسام والأعراض يرى كما هو لا أن يكون من موجبات الرؤية أو من شروطها.
And their making distance between the seer and the seen, and the connection of the ray, and the imprinting of the seen in the eye of the seer, and the removal of veils conditions for vision is invalid. And likewise their condition that the seen be located in space (mutahayyiz) is invalid, because they have conceded to us the vision of accidents, and whoever among them does not concede that we compel him by evidence. And the connection of the ray to accidents is inconceivable, and likewise their perception by contact (mumassa) as we have established. And from the doctrine of al-Nazzam is that the ray is a body that touches the seen, and the accident does not touch, and with it indicates that this is false. And likewise the imprinting of the seen, because the seen does not imprint, but God creates the likeness of the image of the seen in the mirror, and there is no image for accidents. And likewise the removal of veils occurs between bodies; as for accidents, it is not said: the veils are removed unless they are removed from their locus. And we have explained before this that the removal of veils from their loci has no effect on them, and moreover we have explained that the veil has no effect in preventing vision, so its removal has no effect in establishing vision. And likewise their making facing (muqabala) a condition is invalid, because facing is established between bodies; as for accidents, they do not face nor are they faced, and their vision has been established, indicating that it is not a condition, except that it occurs in the case of bodies due to the necessity of their being located and finite; and everything is seen as it is; vision does not necessitate a change in the seen, for the black is seen black and the white is seen white, and likewise the moving and the stationary, the long and the short, the wide, the triangular, the square, the round, the inanimate and the animate, and everything that is beheld and seen from bodies and accidents is seen as it is, not that it is among the necessitations of vision or its conditions.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage refutes several conditions that the Mu'tazila posit for the possibility of vision (ru'ya), specifically regarding the vision of God. The author lists conditions: (1) distance between seer and seen, (2) connection of a ray (shu'a') from the eye to the object, (3) imprinting of the seen in the eye, (4) removal of veils, and (5) the seen being located in space (mutahayyiz). The author declares all these conditions invalid. He argues that the Mu'tazila themselves concede that accidents (a'rad) are visible, and for accidents, these conditions are inapplicable: rays cannot connect to accidents, accidents cannot be touched, they do not have images to imprint, and veils are only between bodies, not accidents. Moreover, the author has previously shown that veils have no effect in preventing vision, so their removal is irrelevant. The condition of facing (muqabala) is also invalid because accidents do not face or are not faced, yet they are seen; facing is only necessary for bodies because they are located and finite. The passage concludes that everything seen—whether bodies or accidents—is seen as it is without change; vision does not impose conditions on the seen object. Thus, the Mu'tazili conditions are not necessary for vision in general, and certainly not for seeing God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision of any object requires physical conditions like distance, a ray, imprinting, removal of veils, facing, or the object being a body.
Correction: Accidents are seen without any of these conditions, proving they are not universal requirements for vision.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the condition of facing (muqabala) invalid for vision?
A) Because God cannot be faced.
B) Because accidents are seen without being faced.
C) Because facing requires distance.
D) Because the Mu'tazila rejected facing.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili conditions for vision are not universal; the visibility of accidents without any of them proves they are not prerequisites for seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself adding hidden conditions to how God should reveal Himself to you—conditions based on your own human expectations? What are they, and are you willing to let them go after seeing how accidents are seen without any of them?
?
The passage says 'everything is seen as it is'—vision does not change the object. When you look at God's creation, do you see it as it truly is, or do you impose your own categories and biases? Which of your assumptions needs to be dismantled first?
?
If God can be seen without any of the physical conditions you normally require for sight, how does that challenge your comfort zone? Are you ready to encounter God on His terms, not yours? What would it mean to surrender that control today?
